The bond market, in this atmosphere of turmoil, is shaken and uncertain, causing widespread caution. In addition, the Suidong rebellion cannot be ignored, and the future feels thorny. The result for the week showed mostly small gains or losses of one or two jiao to four jiao. This month's Ding and Yi classes, however, rose by one and a half jiao to three and a half jiao. Spring and Autumn

Talking About Avoiding the Heat 156

— (Cai Xuanqing)

People who walk and work in the heat all day long are less likely to suffer from heatstroke, while those who leisurely avoid the heat are more susceptible to it. Farmers in the countryside work in the fields all day long, exposed to the scorching sun, sweating profusely, yet they don't seem to suffer from any illness and are even stronger than others. Wealthy families, living in high-rise buildings with electric fans and air conditioning, completely shielded from the heat, feel as if they are in a cool world, no longer aware of the misery of summer heat. If they occasionally have to go out, their bodies, long separated from the heat, are most easily harmed by the summer heat. The rain and sunshine, yin and yang, cold and heat of nature are meant to nurture and temper people and things. Without them, development and growth would feel incomplete. Therefore, there is no need to avoid the heat so much. Recently, envoys from various friendly nations and important figures from our country have flocked to Qingdao, Beidaihe, and Guling, and even more wealthy people have gone to Japan, all seeking to escape the oppressive summer heat. If these are talents relied upon by the nation, of course they should be carefully protected. But let me tell two old stories: During the Song Dynasty, Duke Wei Zhang Jun supervised the army to resist the Jin invaders. His two generals, Shao Hongyuan and Li Xianzong, had long disagreed. Just as a decisive battle was planned, Xianzong heard this and exclaimed loudly, "In this intense heat, it is unbearable even to wave a fan in the coolness, let alone engage in battle!" As a result, the army's morale collapsed, leading to a defeat at Fuliji and a retreat of over two hundred li. During the Qing Dynasty, Zeng Zhongxiang Guo Quan supervised the army to attack Jinling. He led his troops out on the first day of the sixth month and captured the provincial capital by the sixteenth day of the sixth month, fighting fiercely in the sweltering heat for half a month without rest. These two stories show that one sought leisure in the summer heat and failed the nation, while the other endured hardship in the summer heat and achieved success. I write them down as material for gentlemen avoiding the heat to chat about under bean arbors and melon trellises.

Escaping the Heat in Eastern Yin 157

— Shen Zhen

The south wind steams dreams, and the oppressive heat torments people. Sweating in the city buildings, I can no longer bear it, and leisurely imagine the mountains and forests. In the early morning, I lightly set out from Wusong and arrived at Jiaochuan. At the corner of Zhaobao, the morning mist had not yet dispersed, and vaguely, I felt an intention to rise up and overturn the heavens. Winding and turning several times, I reached Yin; after a week in the city, I passed Baochuang on a roundabout. It was a rural market, desolate and unremarkable, but the incense flourished at the Ashoka Temple, opening the path in its midst, and truly holding the lifeline of the mountain families. Hundreds of people live around the temple, and whether they are farmers or laborers, their clothing and food all depend on the mountains. The mountain monks' breathing is revered by all, and no one dares to make noise. Those above and below live in harmony, a hidden paradise. Entering the temple, I was delighted to see its magnificent Buddhist realm, with towering halls. The land was secluded and spacious, without the labor of climbing steep mountains, and the city was hidden without being noisy. Traveling by water was without hardship, making it most suitable for female protectors to form incense alliances, showering the ground with money, and creating a magnificent image in the corner, lasting for a long time without change. However, Cao Xi's scoop, viewed with eyes seeking the secluded and scenic, could not help but feel that entering the mountain was not deep enough, and leaving the secular world was not far enough. Thus, I hurriedly left. Entering Tiantong from Shaobai, the double oars chased the wind, my heart wanted to dissolve. The peaks turned, and the road ahead was completely lost. The sky was dark blue, and the water mirror reflected the green. The small boats brushed past the shadows of the trees, and my sleeves were all blue, as if sitting in heaven. By chance, not for the first time, I sat on the boat and sang: "Layer upon layer of mountain mist connects to Maoyang, double oars break through the waves, passing five villages. A stream circles and opens the earth's veins, four mountains enclose the light of the sky; strangely dressed people catch the eye, forgetting the year of the Jiazi cycle, ten miles of temple depth, monks chant in silence, one almost suspects a farming village exists outside this world." To the east are Maoyang and Lingfeng Mountains, also pure land of the Jetavana Garden. Coming from the five villages, the mountain people are simple and unadorned, straight as a string, running through dozens of miles. In the level fields between the ridges, one occasionally sees ancient clothing and whiskers like molted shells, harmonious above the sounds of Kangqu. The summer heat has been half-cleansed. Leaving the boat, I put on my wax-soled shoes and entered the path to Tiantong, climbing the slopes. Ancient cedars blocked the sky, and chaotic pines lined the road. The thick shade was like water, and the rustling of the pines shook my sleeves, creating a deep coolness. Mountain birds flattered me with their clear sounds, chasing away the summer heat for me. Circling the pine path, light waves passed over my head, pouring down like midday thunder, and my soul dissolved with it. Slightly turning, a huge pool appeared in front of me, a paradise for fish. Crossing the pool, I saw a faint yellow gap among the green shadows, and the temple gate slowly appeared. Secluded and mysterious, it could be said that the Ashoka Temple lay at my feet, as one could not even glimpse a spot a hundred steps away. Entering the gate, I walked around the corridors, and the Buddhist halls were high and spacious, no different from Ashoka's, but between each window and railing, lush greenery filled everything. A faint ray of sunlight passed through, and the wild horses did not leap, a quiet interest entered my heart, and all remaining worries ceased. Sitting in the guest hall, I asked for food, and the abbot spoke in a way that was pleasing to me. Leaving and walking around the mountains, my mood became more relaxed, and clear thoughts slowly emerged, and I no longer thought about leaving the mountain. I rested briefly in the stone forest, the stream purling at my elbow. The old trees called to the dragons, and the cool breeze blew through my hair. The evening birds were silent, and various flowers were on the trees. I closed my eyes and meditated, as if sitting in a hollow mulberry tree. When the wind stopped, the fragrance of flowers was faint, and the clear chanting was faintly heard descending from the sky. The bell rang once, and all sounds were silent. Suddenly waking up, I took my cane and returned to the temple, as if the lingering dream had not yet faded. After dinner, I brewed tea and sought out a monk to chat with. We only talked about the scenery and did not expound on the Dharma. Sitting in the tea-scented room, I straightened my temples, and all thoughts died. Before long, the monk went to bed, and the moon peeped at people, shining like water. My heart floated like a wisp of silk. In the midst of the ethereal, the pine waves suddenly roared, and the dark valleys angrily expanded, touching the empty valley and creating strange sounds. The universe shook, and the small room was filled with ten thousand thoughts. Then, the clear chime broke the dream, and the dawn chanting calmed the heart. I leaped up and awoke, and I did not know how Wen Shu and the pines would be again.

Watching the Waterfall at Laoshan 158

— Zhu Mengqi

Those who travel to famous mountains either sail the sea in advance, or drive by land. Having arrived at the mountain, those who have difficulty walking will be carried in sedan chairs. Sea voyages suffer from wind and waves, and powerful people often use warships to help them cross. Land travel means hiring a car into the mountains, without worrying about bumps. Traveling to the mountains as scheduled for a day or two is like seeing immortals above the clouds, vague and uncertain, and it is difficult to explore the wonders of even a tenth of the mountain.

The day after I visited Diangang Bay, my interest was greatly aroused. I gathered my companions and planned to visit the Taiqing Palace. At that time, it was sometimes cloudy, sometimes rainy, and sometimes sunny. When the car reached Dalao Village, the rain suddenly became heavy, the water vapor was dense, and the clouds and mountains were mistaken for each other. A hundred steps away, the mud was flowing down, and we could not move forward. We were greatly frightened and quickly retreated. Our faces were covered with yellow slurry, and we looked at each other in dismay. The car stopped by the Shahe River. A rapid stream faced us, looking like it was rushing, and when we tested it, it reached our stomachs and was too deep to cross. The men and women of the nearby village came to watch. When asked about visiting the Huayan Temple, they all shook their heads, indicating that it was impossible. If we wanted to travel, we could only hire sedan chairs to cross the water to the Shenqing Palace at Huahualang. Huahualang is a waterfall. I thought that during this rainy season, there would definitely be a spectacle, so I left the car, put on a bamboo hat, and hired a sedan chair. First, we crossed the Shahe River. The river was about five zhang wide, and the current was turbulent. The chair carriers walked like crabs, and the passengers closed their eyes, afraid to look left or right. We arrived at Dalao Temple in the east, rested for a while, and then turned south, winding up to the Shenqing Palace. We sat down for a while, sipped tea, and chatted with the old Taoist about mulberry and hemp. Going down from the Shenqing Palace, the rain had subsided, the clouds were hazy, the sunlight was faintly leaking, the mountains were flowing, and the green was intensified. Those behind saw those in front, and their white clothes were all blue-black. Wild flowers exhaled fragrance, and vines pulled at our skirts. We did not realize that our souls and bones were all green, and we immediately regretted that we could not quickly transform into deer and wild birds. Four or five village girls each peeked through the gaps in the fence, exchanging love songs with the chair carriers. Their cloud-like hair and slender feet were just like the people of thirty years ago. We crossed the Liucun River, entered the mountains, and gradually went deeper, walking among scattered chaotic rocks. The mountain path, which seemed to exist before, became increasingly unattainable as we advanced. We crossed the Liushui River fourteen times, and there were no villages or dog barks to be seen. Black clouds pressed down, and thunder rolled on the rocks. Following the last stream to the east, we zigged and zagged seven or eight times in no more than one or two li. From afar, we heard the loud roar of the waterfall, but we could not find it. After another li or so, a drizzle started again, and the cold was like late autumn. The rain was as fine as mist, and people could not distinguish their clothes face to face. The sedan chair moved very slowly. Suddenly, we heard the people in front shouting: "The waterfall is here!" Looking into the distance, the mountains were like a ring, and the road ahead was blocked. A steep cliff of a thousand ren, and the waterfall poured down from a break in the middle of the ring, fearing more than ten zhang, and also a zhang wide; suspended like white silk, we could not tell where it came from. Tall pine trees grew beside the waterfall, and old vines covered the cliffs, swaying with green and flying, turning our sleeves into a rosy cloud. We got out of the sedan chair and walked against the waterfall in the stream, braving the rain, not bothered by the chaotic rocks and rapid currents. Five zhang away from the waterfall, we could no longer move forward. We sat on a precarious rock, quietly contemplating: That rushing stream, clear and cold, from distant times to now, never resting day or night; I wonder if going out of the mountain is the same as being in the mountain? How can I transform into thousands of bodies, travel through the streams and valleys, and examine what I encounter! Below the waterfall, the water strikes the stones and breaks them into holes, the holes fold into streams, the streams fold into gullies, and into beaches. The reason why there was no path to get here before is probably because of this. Four or five wild children, all four or five years old, strong and healthy like adults, played naked in the spring, just like ducks in the spring river.

When the sun was setting, we urged each other to return. On the way, the sound of the spring was like a gong, like a pipe, like a , like silk , like jade pendants, mixing together, sending us off, along with the sounds of singing and poetry. We returned to Shahe River, and the water had not decreased much. The chair carriers did not choose a shallow place to cross, and the passengers' clothes were all wet. We laughed together without stopping.

We stayed at Li Village, and the sound of the spring was on the pillow.

Escaping the Heat 159

— Tong Heng

Braving the Dangers of Mount Hua 160

— Yan Chonglou

Mount Hua is in my hometown of Shaanxi, but I have lived far away for a long time. It was not until this year that I was able to fulfill my long-cherished wish for a grand tour.

The paths of Mount Hua are famous for their steepness, but what interests me most is the danger of the roads. The "Thousand-Foot Precipice" is a narrow, protruding path sandwiched between two mountains. People must pull themselves up with iron chains and crawl on their knees to move forward! There is also the "Azure Dragon Ridge," which is built on a very long mountain ridge, with no support on either side and a bottomless abyss below! Walking on such a dangerous road, one cannot help but feel their heart pounding! But after passing through it, there is a sense of triumphant joy. There are also places like "Monkey's Worry," "Ladder to Heaven," "Ear-Scraping Cliff," and "Rotten Wooden Rubber," whose names alone can tell you the degree of their danger.

The posture of Mount Hua is majestic and grand. Its height of five thousand zhang may not be the highest record, but when I entered the mountain, every turn brought a change to my eyes, and the changes were so magical that they filled my heart and refreshed my eyes. And the coolness of the mountain is even better for escaping the heat.

The scenery of Mount Hua is beautiful and varied. In front of the mountain, there are winding waterfalls. At the foot of the mountain, there are lush forests. The entire mountain has twenty-four stone caves. On the eastern peak is the "Hua Yue Immortal Palm," one of the eight scenic spots of Chang'an. Tang Dynasty poems: "Jade hibiscus on the immortal's palm" refers to this place. As for historical sites, there are "Laojun Hanging the Plow," "Chen Bo's Chess Pavilion," "Emperor Wu Asks the Way," and "Han Yu Throws a Book," which are too numerous to count.

The sedan chair of Mount Hua is very interesting. It is a round chair with a backrest, with two long bamboo poles on either side. People sit in the chair, leaning tightly against the backrest, and two people lift it up, considering it a tool for walking up the mountain. When going down the mountain, the chair is turned over, with the backrest in front, and the person's chest and abdomen are on the backrest to prevent falling. This kind of mountain chair is called "Hua Gan" by the locals.

There is a path out of Mount Hua, most of which is connected with iron ropes, and one must rely on the iron ropes to climb. There is a local saying: "If you want to find a corpse, go to Shangzhou, Henan." The meaning of these two sentences is that when going up and down the mountain, if you accidentally fall, then you have to go to the neighboring province to find the corpse! In fact, you only need to "step firmly on the ground and be careful every step" (someone has carved these words on the mountain) and there will be no danger. The mountain is divided into five peaks: east, west, south, north, and center. The east, west, and south peaks stand like tripods, and the highest is the south peak. At the top of the south peak, there is a pool of spring water facing the sky, called "Yangtian Pool." I lingered by the pool for half a day, looking down at the peaks below the clouds, and the earth beyond the mountains. For the first time, I felt the vastness that nature has given us.

Ballad of Sedan Chair Carriers in Mount Lu 161

— Shufan

White clouds rise in the mountains, evening cooking smoke rises below, for there are cloud-watchers, a whole summer is enough to live on. Cool breeze's kindness reaches to the bone, don't blow it down to the ground! When it blows, the rivers and lakes grow cold, who will bother to come up the mountain? Heaven's heart divides warmth and coolness, we laborers have our rest, how can we get a generous government official, who pours down the scorching sun all the time! May the mountain be like a bamboo shoot in spring, growing ever taller! Every step is worth gold, and the reed bunting chirps in the green spring.

A Record of Victory on the Southern Peak of Mount Kuang 162

— Shufan

I have long admired the beauty of Mount Lu! Unfortunately, I was burdened by secular affairs and unable to fulfill my wish. By chance, I met Fenfeng, Liu, Mengzhao, Tao Tan, and asked about the Upper Qing ruins. They allowed me to stay for two nights and asked me to be their guide. Thinking that it would only take a day and night to rush from Ying to Mount Kuang, I strongly advocated a side trip. We agreed and set sail from Qiantang, traveling upstream along the Yanxi River, passing through Jiande and Jinlan, and setting foot in the Three Caves. We reviewed our old travels, each one a source of sadness, and I recorded all my thoughts and feelings separately in writing. From Jin entering Gan, the flying boat went against the coolness, and my heart was refreshed. The spirit of those serving on the carriages and horses seemed far inferior to that of half a year ago, and the person in charge should urgently improve this! Along the line, the original fields have been turned into money, showing signs of recovery, truly, governance and livelihood are nurtured, and the people's exhaustion is not to be feared. From there, we went to Nanchang and Jiujiang, followed Lotus Cave, and reached Guling. The stone steps were winding and circling, a total of seventeen miles. Although I was sick and stumbling, the clouds and mist emerging from my elbows were a great spectacle. Guling occupies the center of Mount Kuang, where inns and residences are scattered, and everything is arranged in order, a cool place for high officials and wealthy merchants. It was midsummer, and the sounds of zithers and songs floated from each window at noon, and I immediately forgot the heat. In the evening, the fragrant breeze trailed shadows, and I felt like ascending to immortality. The only problem was that prices were too high, and those below the middle class could not afford to stay long. The most wonderful things in the mountains are water and clouds; streams and waterfalls are everywhere, and daytime is like thunder, this is the wonder of water; without rain, it is misty and without cold, like jade catkins, this is the transformation of clouds. Most of the majestic, secluded, and strange places are on the peaks and in the deep valleys, which Guling does not have. However, cutting paths back and forth, often dozens or hundreds of miles, travelers all abandoned their staffs and shoes, so the price of sedan chairs was particularly high. We believe that exploring the mountains is about suiting one's nature, and our movements are up to us, relying on our own strength; if taking a sedan chair is just making ourselves comfortable, we neither desire nor lack such fortune. We packed our luggage, set out for the secluded southern mountains, headed from the inn to Hankou Gorge, circled around the Red Road, and at the Horizontal Gate, we saw the clouds opening, and the main peaks appeared one by one. After another two miles, we climbed Hanpokou, where the Five Old Peaks towered halfway to the sky, as if wanting to scoop up a spoonful of water from Poyang Lake. South of the ridge, it overlooks the Hukou, and looking to the extreme, the sails are strong, mixing with the chaotic green of the mountains at the tail of Chu, vast and unparalleled. Nearby, sand islets floated in purple, the sunset reflecting, the interest was in a water painting. To the west, the Nine Odd Peaks were like a folding screen, ringed and inserted, bordering the blue sky, and hanging waterfalls, the wind blowing as if cutting off. Slightly east, second only to the Five Old Peaks, were the Taiyi Plowhead Peaks, their stone shapes like fire, burning brightly, standing tall and not yielding to each other. The ridge used to have a pavilion, and now it has been increased to three, a long slope hanging upside down, the tail of Tuanshan. Following the right cliff, we picked our way down, for a total of six thousand five hundred steps, and reached Baihejian; looking back at the waist of Taiyi Peak, another practice was released, the midday thunder roaring angrily, and the silver armor broke through the blue, that is, the Baihuicao flowing from the Three Stone Beams; rushing into the stream, in a turbulent manner. Further down was Bingxian Valley, filled with lush green, unfathomable. We reached Sanxia Stream. The stones were startled as if to run, and the sound of the water was even louder. The streams of Tuanshan and Changlong all converge here, for a total of ninety-nine. The ancients compared its seclusion and danger to the Kui Gorge, hence this name. Crossing the stream, we reached a temple, where Li Bo of the Tang Dynasty read books, the ruined foundation and crumbling walls, the bells and drums were silent; only the ground faced the rushing stream, and the back rested against the mountains, looking up at the beautiful appearance of the Five Old Peaks, especially clear, so the Zhi has the words "a secluded valley that is the best in one mountain." The temple houses Qing Dynasty Luohan portraits, totaling one hundred and eighty-one scrolls. Between the zhang-wide paintings, their actions, postures, smiles, and words, their spirits and forms are complete, interspersed with changes in birds, animals, trees, and stones, making them even more Qing Yan and profound. Unfortunately, Kang Changsu affixed his seal to each one, hoping to pass them on together, which was inevitable! Leaving the temple, we advanced along the stream and arrived at Yuyuan Pond, a , and the water vapor was awe-inspiring. There are many stone carvings around the pool, and more than half of them are blurred, but Mi Fu's poem can still be read, and Zhang Xiaoxiang's inscription of the two characters "Yujian" is still vigorous as before. Further south, the stones all bind the stream, becoming high and low, the blue walls rising and falling sharply, forming banks and bottoms; thus marveling at the craftsmanship of the creator! We reached Qixian Bridge, cutting off a large ravine, blocking the angry , and the shock almost made us unable to hold ourselves. The bridge was built in the seventh year of the Song Dynasty's Xiangfu period, and although the valleys have changed, the pillars are still intact, and the firmness of the structure is evident. Tracing back from Baihejian to here, we have crossed three rainbows, so it is also called Three Stone Beams. To the east of the bridge is a spring called Zhaoyin, which Lu Yu once rated as the sixth best in the world. At that time, the green sun was falling to the west, and the peaks were slowly turning into clouds, like cooking pots lined up. We planned to explore more of the wonders of Wanshan and Xiufeng, but when we asked the locals, they all stuck out their tongues and said: It's getting late, you won't be able to return! We had no choice but to return the way we came, the steps were steep, facing us, and we had to raise our feet to our chests, making it twice as difficult as going down the mountain; our withered feet exhausted and sweating, we finally reached Hanpokou, and looking at the lake waves changing in the setting sun, we were once again happy and forgot our form.

Examples of Experimental Treatment for Nocturnal Emission 168

— Dr. Dai De

Funeral Before Death 177

— Me

In South Carolina, USA, there is a 73-year-old woman named Fen Duo Peike who recently suffered from a serious illness and was dying. She knew that she had no hope of survival, so she asked to hold her funeral early before her death so that she could truly receive it. At the time of the funeral, the old lady also made a short speech, expressing her deep gratitude to all the relatives and friends at the Mecca funeral. Three days after the funeral, she died. Such a funeral is unheard of, but I say this old lady is really interesting.

No one can escape the moment of life and death. As for being dead, you are completely unaware. No matter how sad, how memorialized, or how grand the ceremony is for your children, grandchildren, relatives and friends, it has nothing to do with the deceased. Therefore, the most sensational funeral of Sheng Xingcun in Shanghai in the past can be called the most grand of the time. However, at that time, someone said that if Sheng Xingcun was sitting on the coffin, or sitting in another carriage (there were no cars at that time) and walking in the middle of the road, it would be a great victory. If the person is dead, this kind of action is originally empty, and you can only see banknotes flying all over the road.

Originally, holding a big funeral and a big memorial service was something that the descendants did for others to see, not a truly filial act for the deceased. This kind of action originally has three functions: one is to show how filial the descendants are to their parents and ancestors, one is to show how阔绰 the descendants are, and one is to show how much wealth the descendants have. The extravagant funeral is extremely luxurious, and the result is nothing more than this, which has nothing to do with the deceased.

Usually, no matter how unfilial or disobedient you are, if your parents die, there will definitely be sentences such as "not dying by yourself, causing misfortune to a certain relative" and "wearing mourning clothes and being confused, crying incoherently" in the public announcement to relatives and friends. This old lady saw through this point and insisted on enjoying it before she died, not after she died. Such a thorough method is indeed rare. Her intention is that if you want to be a filial son, show it to me before I die, not to others after I die. If you want to fulfill the friendship of relatives and friends, you should also show it to me before I die, not to others after I die. This old lady is really reasonable.

But I say that although this method is good, it is never a good idea to have a funeral before you die. You will also feel embarrassed. In my opinion, it is better to celebrate your birthday than to have a funeral. I think this old lady must have forgotten the method of celebrating her birthday when she was seventy-six or seventy years old. Assuming that she celebrates her birthday, she can still let her descendants do their filial piety, let the guests be full, and sit in the middle and receive people's congratulatory gifts. Isn't this more interesting than having a big funeral?

The Bashkin Couple 178

— Makino

"Oh, what a feud, what terrible hatred is this? Beating like that! Listen... Oh... Heavens, what a sin, aren't they husband and wife? ... What on earth is it all about?... Like this..."

Old Peterovna cried out, as she hurriedly ran down the three flights of stairs, she ran to

..., Have you all seen it? The couple were twisting together desperately, looking just like two dogs fighting... Like that..." Peterovna shouted hoarsely to everyone, her eyes wide, she constantly lowered her head sharply, peering at everyone from the edge of her old glasses with a faint gaze, as if searching for someone; her hands trembled violently in front of her chest from excessive stimulation, sometimes tightly clutching her clothes, tearing at them; her face was pale, "...Beating like that... Oh... What a sin, I tried to persuade them but failed, instead adding fuel to the fire... That drunken Bashkin grabbed Alina's beautiful hair tightly with one hand... Like this... Like this... His other hand was clenched into a terrible fist, pounding her back again and again... Like drumming! Oh!... Poor Alina, she was hitting him, but... she was so weak!... She didn't inflict any pain on the brutal Bashkin at all!... You... Listen!... This is... this..."

Then everyone followed Peterovna's gaze, focusing on the windows on the third floor, but what could they see? They only heard the thudding of fists on flesh, and the sounds of messy and heavy footsteps, everyone looked up nervously, some also poked their heads out onto the balcony. Here, Peterovna swept past everyone like a whirlwind, and then she hurriedly ran back to the small building.

Before long, Peterovna ran down again, her face full of joy, she said: "Oh!... Merciful God!... God has given them happiness - they are reconciled again!..." Saying this, she made the sign of the cross on her chest.

What an interesting old Russian woman!

Since I had just moved into Shuocun, I was particularly interested in these Russians who were previously unfamiliar to me. I used to observe them carefully in my spare time. Their faces unique to Russians, their strange noses, some women still wearing half-old red and green clothes... all of this gave me new stimulation. Decades ago, weren't they all noble royalty? Their royal dignity has also been shattered.

Later, gradually, I became friends with the kind old woman Peterovna. One day, I asked about the Bashkin couple, and she began to chatter endlessly like all old women:

"Oh!... What a sin, it's really impossible to say, this... I don't know how God listened to the devil and drove us out of the land of Russia, handing everything over to the crude workers, foolish farmers, and all the low-class things... Oh!

You know, we are all royalty, relatives of Saint Nicholas, we used to not have to do anything, not even walk... but now, we have to make a living for ourselves! This is a torture for us... How is that possible!...

Bashkin, poor Bashkin couple!... He never had a job, and when he ran out of the money left by his old mother, he fell into poverty forever!... God gave him a violent personality. Drinking, gambling wildly... and an arrogance that shouldn't have existed anymore, oh! This poor young man!

I don't know from what day they started losing breakfast... Later, sometimes they couldn't get anything to eat all day, not even a small slice of black bread! Bashkin would go crazy and beat his wife Alina... Like that, he was as if fighting her! What was going on?

Borrowing money was unsuccessful, no one would give them any help!

One night, Bashkin didn't come back. When I went to the balcony to cool off, a man's laughter filled his room! What a sin, I... Please God forgive me, I peeked through the keyhole like that, ah!... A, a strange man was hugging the beautiful Alina!... God knows, what were they doing! ... Later... Later, strange men often slept with Alina... Poor Bashkin sometimes spent an entire night alone on the lonely streets... Alina was happy to do that! Sometimes she cried in shame... The sinful Bashkin even drank up the money that was hard to come by.

Oh... They fought almost every few days, that was when Alina's hard-earned money was used up by him!..."

But, after a while, the beautiful Alina disappeared, and naturally there were no more fights! Later, I heard Peterovna say: "She was probably sold by him!"

Filial Son Afu 179

— Cheng Xiuyang

After a rain, swarms of dragonflies circled in the sky, the neighbor's children used cattail fans tied to the ends of bamboo poles, fighting back and forth to catch them, so I thought of Afu, the farmhand in my family.

Afu had special skills in catching dragonflies. After he caught them, he tied their heads with yarn, always a string of more than a dozen, because they restrained each other, they looked very beautiful when they flew. Afu has always been unforgettable in my mind, probably for this reason.

He was forthright, strong, and never lazy. In addition to the work in the fields, he was willing to grow vegetables and carry water, and we didn't have to take care of him. From the time he got up to the time he went to bed every day, he liked to drink a few mouthfuls of wheat wine in the corner of the threshing ground at dusk, and he never rested. I remember one year during the New Year, when settling wages, my grandfather told him with a smile.

"Afu, you already have more than thirty yuan now. I'll help you make it forty yuan and put it in interest. In the future, when you marry a wife, you can have a good family."

Afu thanked him with his head down, and then said his wish in a low voice:

"Sir, how much does a piece of land for a grave cost? My father hasn't been buried yet."

From then on, the filial son Afu became famous in the town. But his companions often joked with him:

"Afu, working hard as a farmhand, not marrying a wife for enjoyment, but buying a grave for your father, you're really a good boy."

Saying this, he patted Afu's strong back, as if an old man loved his son.

"Afu, marrying a wife is also being a filial son, did you hear the master (private tutor) say? Of the three unfilial acts, the greatest is having no descendants." Another companion proudly recited two refined lines, circling his head, still afraid that Afu didn't understand, explaining: "Your father raised you, and you should also raise a son to pass on the lineage. Understand? That's being a filial son."

That companion even made obscene gestures with his fingers.

Afu sat cross-legged in the corner of the threshing ground like a Maitreya, holding a small bottle of wheat wine in his hand, drinking mouth to mouth, while taking out broad beans from his pocket to peel and eat. Hearing his companions teasing him, he spat out a mouthful of phlegm.

"Shameless!" Afu said to that companion, "Wife, wife, might as well hug a pillow for fun at night!"

In the cotton fields and by the riverbanks, young men often flirted with girls, the four-line hill songs (country love songs) were sung loudly to each other, a kind of instinctive desire in the countryside was thoroughly unreserved. But Afu always pretended to be serious and never said a joke to the girls.

Grandfather secretly promised Afu a wish, he saved up his wages to buy a grave to bury his father, grandfather prepared to pay attention to a good girl to help him make a family. But unfortunately, in the vortex of the Great Revolution, my family was robbed, the houses were set on fire by local gangsters, and grandfather failed to escape and died. After the chaos subsided, my family's land changed its surname, and naturally they couldn't afford to hire a farmhand. When Afu went to the city to find work, he said to my father;

"When the young master farms in the future, Afu will still come to be a farmhand."

Father could only smile bitterly. Afu was already like family in our house, and I was even more reluctant to see him leave suddenly. Father finally gave him some pocket money, and said sadly:

"Afu, the thing the old master promised you can only be in vain, you should go to the city to find a master, and you'll have to marry a wife in the future."

Afu didn't spend a year in the city before news came that he had married a widow, and was tricked by a matchmaker out of the hard-earned money he had saved up to buy a grave to bury his father. Father sighed when he heard this and said, "The city is not a good place!"

That matter has been twenty years ago now. Last year, a letter came from home saying that Afu had returned to the town again. He had completely changed his appearance. Twenty years of life's oppression had completely changed him. He often borrowed money from his father by force, or stole things to buy wine to drink. In August, when picking cotton, he raped a little girl in the field and was tied up by the town and sent to the city, where he was sentenced to twelve years in prison.

A filial son Afu who hated women, finally committed rape and was imprisoned. I can never figure out the contradictory cause of his crime. I want to have a chance to go back to my hometown to see him once.

A Soldier Selling a Watch 180

— Hu Weixiong

In the afternoon of June, the sun exploded in the sky like a bomb, the stone road evaporated with a scorched smell. This is a narrow street in a corner of a big city.

Page 20

, with three or five rickshaws passing by to hand over shifts at the rickshaw depot, and a group of children naked from the waist up running around in the street, embellishing the desolate market.

The only big shop on this street is Sun Erpangzi's butcher shop. He habitually closes the market after noon. In fact, no customer comes to his shop in the afternoon to do business worth three or five hundred copper coins. He put his old rattan chair under the eaves and slept his afternoon nap with his black-haired chest open.

The busiest business is Zhu Houzi's tobacco shop, where three copper coins buy kerosene, and a box of incense matches can be exchanged for two cigarettes. From the opening of the market in the morning to the closing of the gate, there are always customers coming and going in front of his shop. The author specially writes about these two shops because they are the most outstanding facades on this narrow street. In addition, they are all sloping low houses, and when people with relatively tall statures pass by, their heads are suspended on the roof.

But Zhu Houzi always envies Sun Erpangzi for having good luck, only doing business for a few hours in the morning, and then sleeping soundly in the afternoon, while he jumps up and down in the shop like his name. He makes a circle with his fingers - fifteen o'clock. The afternoon should be relatively idle, but he can't take a nap at the counter, or leave the shop for a few minutes. This excellent tobacco shop has not hired even a basic shop assistant.

Isn't there a customer coming now? It is a middle-aged soldier wearing a sauce-colored tattered military uniform. He dropped a copper coin and bought two cigarettes "produced by our shop". He seems to have not smoked cigarettes for several days, and burned most of a cigarette in one breath as soon as he lit it, but no trace of green smoke came out of his nostrils; with another mouthful, a cigarette had already burned his fingers. He glanced at Zhu Houzi, barely making a smile, but no one knew the reason for his smile.

"Boss," he finally opened his mouth: "Do you want to buy something?"

Zhu Houzi looked at him. He was surprised that the soldier might be a mental patient, and he was also wary that he would suddenly jump over the counter and rob something. Zhu Houzi had that kind of experience. But no, he surveyed the street for a week and took out a gold watch from his waistband and placed it on the counter.

"Boss, I don't have any money to use. I have to sell this watch cheaply."

Zhu Houzi's eyeballs were rolling in his eye sockets. He knew what "kind of person" the soldier was. He snatched the watch over and listened to it by his ear. The sound was very loud, and the outer shell was shining with gold color under the sunlight. Zhu Houzi knew that even if it wasn't gold, it was a good watch.

"How much do you want to sell it for?" His tone was like a pawnbroker.

"Boss, how much money do you want to give me?" The soldier smiled reluctantly again? "I haven't smoked for two days!"

Seeing that the boss was staying put as if he didn't understand his words, he immediately added:

"I haven't smoked opium for two days!"

Zhu Houzi was really smiling in his heart. This unexpected deal was worth half a month's business, but he deliberately examined the watch, and then pried it open to inspect the internal parts, shaking his head:

"No way! The internal parts have all been moved." Like an expert, "I'll put six cents here."

At this time, that group of children suddenly saw the soldier, "A soldier," they shouted, slowly walking towards Zhu Houzi's shop, making him even more uneasy.

"Boss, add a little more, this watch can be pawned for two yuan. - Hehe, the boss is a smart man, just add a little more."

Zhu Houzi held the watch tightly in his hand. He was thinking about sending him away quickly. In any case, he wouldn't lose money at two yuan. The group of children would talk nonsense when they saw it. He turned around and picked out two banknotes from the locked silver box, and went back to the counter to give them to him, deliberately raising his voice:

"Hey, two cigarettes."

The soldier took the banknotes, hurriedly stuffed them into his pocket, and walked towards the center of the street.

Zhu Houzi closed the market early that day, and admired the gold watch under the lamp for a long time before wandering to Sun Erpangzi's shop to watch beating five hundred spades.

"Little Houzi is early today." Sun Er started to get up.

Zhu Houzi saw that the time had come, and he deliberately slowly took out the gold watch from his chest pocket. When he looked at the hour hand, it was still pointing at five o'clock. He immediately put it to his ear, but there was no sound at all. He shook it hard, and he only heard a few clicks like a dripping faucet and then stopped. Zhu Houzi was so anxious that his face turned red. Two banknotes were swaying in front of his eyes. He ran out of Sun Erpangzi's shop, wanting to chase the soldier who deserved to be beheaded back.

The street lamps were vaguely hanging on the telephone poles, and the children wearing around were gone. Zhu Houzi felt like he had fallen into an ice cellar. Two yuan, two yuan, he was almost crying.

Since the Swallow Society held its inaugural meeting at Xinghualou, it has expanded internally and divided into four groups: singing group, harmonica group, drama group, and Peking opera group. Currently, there are singing and harmonica groups broadcasting. The Central District practice location of the society has been set at Room 310, 3rd Floor, Taichang Building, North Zhejiang Road. The Central District practice time is Wednesday, Saturday, and Sunday, from 2:00 PM to 5:00 PM.

The Moonlight Music Research Society's harmonica team has now expanded and is recruiting members. If you are interested in the harmonica and are determined to join the society, please send your address, name, and harmonica experience to Chen Tongfu, No. 24 Haige Lane, Haige Road.

The Times Art Society is now jointly organizing the Qingbai Drama Society and the Modern Society to form a drama group to broadcast "Remarriage".

Mr. Ma Loufen is now planning a new plan and needs a large number of female members and music team members (instruments are self-provided). Movie

"The Wayward Lamb" Cast List 184

This year's blockbuster from Lianhua Film Company, Cai Chusheng's next masterpiece after "Song of the Fishermen," "The Wayward Lamb," is now premiering at the Jin Cheng Grand Theatre in Shanghai. It has been in preparation for over a year, in production for a year, and cost tens of thousands of dollars. Its excellence and grand scale are unprecedented in Chinese films. Below is a description of the roles played by the more important actors in the film, as well as their characters' personalities, so that viewers may appreciate the touching plot and the greatness of the work.

Ge Zuozhi as Little Sanzi: A kind, poor village child, full of enthusiasm and a sense of justice. But like most Chinese, he suffers from timidity. In the village education that makes people more foolish, he becomes a mischievous child. Furthermore, due to the oppression of natural and man-made disasters, he becomes a wild street child. However, he still retains some of his kind nature, although he struggles daily in a miserable life.

Chen Juanjuan as Cuier: An uncultured wild girl from the countryside. On the surface, she is brave, impetuous, and willful, but her heart contains a passionate tenderness peculiar to women. She has the same experiences as Sanzi, but her environment has not changed her nature.

Li Zhuozhuo as Shen: An urban woman who worships so-called modern civilization, possessing a snake-like allure. In a material enjoyment and a certain unsatisfying environment, she constantly seeks new stimulation. Her life is decadent and contradictory.

Zheng Junli as the Old Servant: A passionate and nervous old servant, with so-called "loyalty and righteousness" and so-called "old-fashioned enthusiasm." Due to the circumstances of his life, his shortsightedness allows him to only touch the surface of facts, without the ability to make further criticisms. This creates his superficiality and contradictions, and ultimately disillusionment. His existence may or may not be, but he is a character assumed by the author to illustrate that the problem of "The Wayward Lamb" cannot be solved from a humanitarian standpoint, without a doubt.

Shen Baining as Shen Cihang: He is a so-called pragmatist and "outstanding talent." Because of his skillful manipulation, he has become revered as a "savior of all" in the metropolis. However, in reality, he is a "[?]." Despite his outward appearance of "nobility and solemnity," his private life is generally unbearable to hear about! A comical character with a dual personality.

Zhang Zhizhi as the Bodyguard: A watchdog kept by a certain type of person. In his memory, there is no justice, he has lost his soul, and he relies solely on cruelty to maintain his food and clothing. He is a typical figure who "helps the wicked to do evil."

Yin Xiucen as the Lunchbox Delivery Man: A pitiful wretch from the petty bourgeoisie, whose life is filled with gray and despised by the "upper class." But when he becomes a so-called nouveau riche by sheer chance, the "upper class" rush to befriend him as a "god of wealth"! He never wants to mention his "shabby" past, because he is no longer a "clumsy oaf" but has a "fortunate appearance"!

Qin Haiyou as the Naughty Boy: A standard figure created by poor rural education, naughty and cunning. He finds pleasure in destroying other people's actions. He has innate intelligence, but this intelligence has been led astray.

Wang Ganqing as Little Zui: A street urchin struggling on the brink of starvation. Every day, every hour, every moment, he has no food to eat. Every day, every hour, every moment, he wants to put the food he sees into his stomach. This habit sometimes gives him an advantage, and sometimes it causes him great losses. A chronic sufferer of habits of daily life.

Hong Jingling as the Village Teacher: The monkey king in the village temple, a pedantic teacher. The dryness of life makes him understand even more deeply the "clear instruction" that "a strict teacher produces good students." However, he often falls asleep due to old age, which inevitably leads to "closed ears and blocked vision," and he ends up beating the innocent. His "dignity" as a teacher is thus turned into an object of ridicule by cunning students.

Li Junqing as the Principal: A sanctimonious principal who regards himself as maintaining public morals. He is the executor of utilitarian education and a glorifier of special classes.

Liu Qiong as Ren Temeng: A so-called young gentleman in the metropolis, possessing all the conditions for pleasing the opposite sex. He is the object of pursuit by so-called modern ladies and a plaything they use to pass their idle time. Wen Rong as Little Sanzi's Father: A farmer struggling in a miserable life, hardworking and upright, but ignorant. In the end, under personal resistance, he perishes like a tiny worm.

Huang Yunzhen as the Noblewoman: A busybody so-called noblewoman, whose only instinct is to be opportunistic. Therefore, she sometimes forgets her original "status" in a loss of composure.
